  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6d7b5f49-4fb3-465e-808f-80205d09af3c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8440153689390384383"><i>JPod</i>, Douglas Coupland's most acclaimed novel to date, is a lethal joyride into today's new breed of tech worker. Ethan Jarlewski and five co-workers whose surnames begin with "J" are bureaucratically marooned in jPod, a no-escape architectural limbo on the fringes of a massive Vancouver game design company. The jPodders wage daily battle against the demands of a boneheade</span>
  <span id="freeText8440153689390384383" style="display:none"><i>JPod</i>, Douglas Coupland's most acclaimed novel to date, is a lethal joyride into today's new breed of tech worker. Ethan Jarlewski and five co-workers whose surnames begin with "J" are bureaucratically marooned in jPod, a no-escape architectural limbo on the fringes of a massive Vancouver game design company. The jPodders wage daily battle against the demands of a boneheaded marketing staff, who daily torture employees with idiotic changes to already idiotic games. Meanwhile, Ethan's personal life is shaped (or twisted) by phenomena as disparate as Hollywood, marijuana grow-ops, people-smuggling, ballroom dancing, and the rise of China. <i>JPod</i>'s universe is amoral, shameless, and dizzyingly fast-paced like our own. <br /><br />Praise for JPod: "JPod is a sleek and necessary device: the finely tuned output of an author whose obsolescence is thankfully years away."-New York Times Book Review"It's to [Coupland's] credit that in JPod he's still nimble enough to take the post-modern man-too young for Boomer nostalgia and too old for youthful idealism-and drown his sorrows in a willful, joyful satire that revels in the same cultural conventions that it sends up."-Rocky Mountain News <br />"It's time to admire [Coupland's] virtuoso tone and how he has refined it over 11 novels. The master ironist just might redefine E. M. Forster's famous dictate 'Only connect' for the Google age."-USA Today <br />"Zeitgeist surfer Douglas Coupland downloads his brain into JPod."-Vanity Fair <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8440153689390384383" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_141304547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141304547" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4820973-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588917773p2/4820973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/141304547?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/4820973-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4820973-nate?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4820973-nate?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4820973-nate?shelf=douglas-coupland">douglas-coupland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141304547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11623695822991849189">Here's what I've noticed. People that have read extensive Coupland novels view this book more negatively. People who have never/barely read Coupland love this book. <br/><br/>It is my opinion that this is a very good book. It's sarcastic, witty, satirical, and outlandish. This didn't come from the same Coupland that wrote Generation X, Microserfs, Shampoo Planet and all those 90's classics that Couplanders want to hang on to so badly. This is a funny book that can be taken lightly with great humor. In tho</span>
  <span id="freeText11623695822991849189" style="display:none">Here's what I've noticed. People that have read extensive Coupland novels view this book more negatively. People who have never/barely read Coupland love this book. <br /><br />It is my opinion that this is a very good book. It's sarcastic, witty, satirical, and outlandish. This didn't come from the same Coupland that wrote Generation X, Microserfs, Shampoo Planet and all those 90's classics that Couplanders want to hang on to so badly. This is a funny book that can be taken lightly with great humor. In those 90's he focused much more on the frustrations and anxieties of his characters. In this book he's almost making those anxieties into absurd hyperbole. For God sakes he inserts himself into the book as a pretentious arrogant asshole that is hated (at first) by everybody in Jpod. <br /><br />If you've never read Coupland before, Read This. If you've read some or lot's of him before, try and laugh. Not every book needs a profound moral of a story. Sometimes you can have some fun reading too. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11623695822991849189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2041095" />
    <a title="Marina Keenan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/134616-marina-keenan"><img alt="Marina Keenan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208453322p2/134616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781596911048" href="/book/show/22538.JPod">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2041095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6783962543594921547">I am a fan of Coupland and I have read everything he has ever published, but it is clear after reading Jpod that he's been in Vancouver too long and needs to get out for a weekend, if only to try another city's dope and take-out. Jpod is supposed to be the sequel to Microserfs, but Coupland wastes this one hunting-and-pecking for Gen Y/Echo Boom culture like a noob coder; he doesn't see that the map is not the terrain. So what if the main character's Mom is growing and selling weed, Dad is datin</span>
  <span id="freeText6783962543594921547" style="display:none">I am a fan of Coupland and I have read everything he has ever published, but it is clear after reading Jpod that he's been in Vancouver too long and needs to get out for a weekend, if only to try another city's dope and take-out. Jpod is supposed to be the sequel to Microserfs, but Coupland wastes this one hunting-and-pecking for Gen Y/Echo Boom culture like a noob coder; he doesn't see that the map is not the terrain. So what if the main character's Mom is growing and selling weed, Dad is dating his son's classmates, and his boss is being manipulated by a billionaire Asian criminal? Sure it's shocking, but is this the zeitgeist, or just, "Extreme Vancouver?" Coupland needs to look beyond the electronic globe his own character creates (he writes himself into the book and creates an interactive-globe killer app that saves the group from techie overwork and under-compensation); that metaphor for an impersonal electronic world fails because his characters never break through as real people with any purpose. Gen Y's struggle to assert "authorship", i.e. identity in a world where society = electronic gaming, should have been inherent in the characters' personal lives and struggles; instead, Coupland builds them with thin and obvious devices like everyone playing the "what-superpower-would-you-have" game. In the end, I am not sure this story or these characters matter, and I think they may as well not have been. Phooey, Doug, Phoeey I say! Has the well run dry? </span>
  <a data-text-id="6783962543594921547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_31919848" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31919848" />
    <a title="Marcus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1439371-marcus"><img alt="Marcus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491400p2/1439371.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31919848?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marcus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marcus" href="/user/show/1439371-marcus">Marcus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31919848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11716412071620946053">One star was generous, trust me. <br/><br/>It's hard to believe that this is the man who authored the book that made me want to become a writer. He wrote three brilliant books, at the beginning of his career, his last being Microserfs...Then I watched him fall shorter and shorter with each subsequent release. <br/><br/>He became a watered down version of his former self with each new book published, not unlike the de-evolution of sitcom characters who become caricatures of their original concept, left with only the</span>
  <span id="freeText11716412071620946053" style="display:none">One star was generous, trust me. <br /><br />It's hard to believe that this is the man who authored the book that made me want to become a writer. He wrote three brilliant books, at the beginning of his career, his last being Microserfs...Then I watched him fall shorter and shorter with each subsequent release. <br /><br />He became a watered down version of his former self with each new book published, not unlike the de-evolution of sitcom characters who become caricatures of their original concept, left with only the qualities most praised by the masses. Shallow versions of their former selves. <br /><br />The same can be said for Coupland's Jpod. This book is full of self-absorbed pretension. He does his very best to imitate a great author (young Douglas Coupland) but his attempts at bizarre situations are failed, and his inclusion of himself as a character in his book raises the self-aggrandizing bar to new heights (or lows, depending on your view)<br /><br />He wastes page after page with binary codes, and random words that have little to no relevance to the "story", taking up space, and raising the page count and price of the book.<br /><br />It seems as if he was merely attempting to recreate Gen X and Microserfs with different names for the characters. <br /><br />This book was so bad, I wouldn't even be willing to trade it, sell it or give it away as I could never do something so malicious to another literate human being.<br /><br />So there it is. One man wrote one of the best books I've ever read, and one of the worst. It may be time to wash my hands of his work completely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11716412071620946053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_21470572" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21470572" />
    <a title="N" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/121445-n"><img alt="N" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529142264p2/121445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21470572?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="N" class="user" itemprop="url" name="N" href="/user/show/121445-n">N</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/121445-n?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21470572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6378127738185562382">I'm a little conflicted over my reaction to this novel. Because it was an undeniably enjoyable read and there was literally not a chapter which didn't make me laugh out loud. And yet, I feel... underwhelmed.<br/><br/>I feel I should preface this review by saying, Coupland is my favourite author. Like, by a really long way. I love all his books insanely much. (Well, except <i>Shampoo Planet</i>. No one loves <i>Shampoo Planet</i>. Except Ryan Ross, <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Fever_You_Can't_Sweat_Out' rel='nofollow noopener'>apparently</a>.) I want to write like him. I would happily only ever read Co</span>
  <span id="freeText6378127738185562382" style="display:none">I'm a little conflicted over my reaction to this novel. Because it was an undeniably enjoyable read and there was literally not a chapter which didn't make me laugh out loud. And yet, I feel... underwhelmed.<br /><br />I feel I should preface this review by saying, Coupland is my favourite author. Like, by a really long way. I love all his books insanely much. (Well, except <i>Shampoo Planet</i>. No one loves <i>Shampoo Planet</i>. Except Ryan Ross, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Fever_You_Can't_Sweat_Out" rel="nofollow noopener">apparently</a>.) I want to write like him. I would happily only ever read Coupland-esque books, if enough existed.<br /><br />That said, what I admire most about his writing is how he has matured. Because I am not one of those people who falls over themselves at how AWESOME <i>Generation X</i> is. It's not. It's a jumping off point -- and the depth, the humanity Coupland has put into his novels in the past 15 years make it look... paltry. <i>Eleanor Rigby</i> completely, completely blew me away. I was expecting to hate it, but its beauty is so understated and genuinely <i>poignant</i>. Seriously: wow.<br /><br />Which is why I was hesitant when I read the description of <i>jPod</i>. It sounded like <i>Microserfs</i> updated and remixed with <i>All Families Are Psychotic</i>. Which it basically is. And don't get me wrong, it's quite a feat to be as <i>successfully</i> zeitgeisty and funny as a <i>Microserfs</i> update calls for. But compared to <i>Eleanor Rigby</i>? Feels undeniably like a step backwards.<br /><br />It was also the first Coupland (barring <i>Shampoo Planet</i> :P) that felt repetitive to me. I've met Ethan before. (In fact, it just took me 30 seconds to remember his name, he's so familiar... as Richard, as Dan.) The romance between Ethan and Kaitlin, in particular, I barely felt like I had to concentrate on, because I knew <i>exactly</i> what was going to happen. Neurotic Boy + Quirky Girl = Cute, Functional Relationship. It's not a bad trope, but it's still a trope (<u>Garden State</u>, anyone?). It's also extremely familiar in the Coupland universe.<br /><br />Confession time: Coupland novels tend to make me cry. Really a lot. <i>Girlfriend In A Coma</i> is the worst, but <i>Microserfs</i> also makes me sob. <i>jPod</i>, even though it rationalized this, felt emotionally void. I wasn't left with any kind of strong emotional connection to any of the characters. And frankly, the warm characterization is what has always separared Coupland from the Bret Easton Ellises of the writing world. (I can't abide Bret Easton Ellis. He's an extraordinarily talented writer. Hate his books.)<br /><br />I took a Contemporary Literature class a couple of years ago and my very smart lecturer was talking, in passing, about Coupland and said that he was the kind of author to write himself into his books. My reaction was, <i>pffft! bitch, please</i>. But I guess she was right; I was wrong. :/ Although metafiction is a pet peeve of mine, I really don't mind it when it's done in the right measure with the right amount of irony. <i>jPod</i> certainly wasn't Philip Roth's <i>Operation Shylock</i> (which I felt fell headfirst into its own wankery), and Coupland-as-a-Machiavellian-tycoon certainly made me laugh a lot. Still, it makes me twitch a little. <br /><br />I feel like this is the book a lot of people (the people who think <i>Generation X</i> is AWESOME and never bothered to read much else of his work) have been expecting from Coupland. The book that's smarter-than-thou and wittily postmodern. And to his credit, he wrote it on his own terms and made it very funny. I just hope he gets back to writing with the heart of <i>Girlfriend In A Coma</i> and <i>Eleanor Rigby</i> in future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6378127738185562382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1445267063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1445267063" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4821718-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393297164p2/4821718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1445267063?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/4821718-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=witty">witty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=biji">biji</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1445267063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer914336819177061948">Recently, a new friend introduced me to the concept of biji, a Chinese form of writing roughly translated as ‘brush notes’ or ‘jottings’. This literary genre is defined by a three-part division consisting of different styles and lacking definitive structure. I sought an example of the format and was directed toward Douglas Coupland’s ‘JPod’, an off-beat story of life in a modern Vancouver office.<br/><br/>Meet Ethan, a 28 year old games developer who spends his days toggling between virtual world building</span>
  <span id="freeText914336819177061948" style="display:none">Recently, a new friend introduced me to the concept of biji, a Chinese form of writing roughly translated as ‘brush notes’ or ‘jottings’. This literary genre is defined by a three-part division consisting of different styles and lacking definitive structure. I sought an example of the format and was directed toward Douglas Coupland’s ‘JPod’, an off-beat story of life in a modern Vancouver office.<br /><br />Meet Ethan, a 28 year old games developer who spends his days toggling between virtual world building, nicknaming co-workers, and fetching the company snack supplies. In his down time, he helps his family dispose of drug dealers, sever ties with psychotic lovers, and feed illegal immigrants squatting in his apartment.<br /><br />After a ruling from new boss Steve, Ethan and his five pod mates must overhaul their current project to accommodate a turtle modelled on a reality T.V. presenter. They decide to corrupt the game with a rogue clown demon, stopping work only to deal with incestuous sexual emergencies, lesbian cult members, and the suspicious abduction of a recently appointed superior.<br /><br />Obligated to undertake a rescue mission in China, Ethan fights off the threat of a viral outbreak, and begs a cantankerous author to rescue him from an early roadside death. Here, Coupland openly inserts himself into the narrative as a character that, although grumpy and not averse to sneaky blackmail, conveniently turns up to save the day.<br /><br />‘JPod’ reads like a scrapbook, shifting between witty narratives, streams of consciousness in squashed or inflated text, character constructed interviews, anagram definitions, and the entire number series of Pi. The biji style is used as a visual representation of life with its interruptions and conflicting demands on attention. Instead of finding the strange digressions distracting, the reader bounces around a lively story full of entertaining, and somehow relevant tangents. <br /><br />Coupland has created a modern tapestry, one I can only describe as an absolute circus. My usual enjoyment of a book lies in how much I miss it after the final page. And still, every morning at reading time, I wish Ethan and the gang were still going strong. It was the first of Coupland’s novels I read and it certainly won’t be the last.<br /><br />Note: I’ve been told there is a T.V. spin-off which I’ve ordered via Amazon. Stay tuned for my thoughts<br /><br />Follow me on the blog! ---&gt; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://ponderdeeper.wordpress.com/">https://ponderdeeper.wordpress.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="914336819177061948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_185800903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185800903" />
    <a title="Joel Bradshaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3344624-joel-bradshaw"><img alt="Joel Bradshaw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601492354p2/3344624.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185800903?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joel Bradshaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joel Bradshaw" href="/user/show/3344624-joel-bradshaw">Joel Bradshaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Joel by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5270146-anna">Anna</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344624-joel-bradshaw?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344624-joel-bradshaw?shelf=catalogged">catalogged</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185800903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15927247820485514550">It's been a while since I've read this book, so we'll see how well this goes. But this book...is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1886.Douglas_Coupland' title='Douglas Coupland' rel='nofollow noopener'>Douglas Coupland</a>. I don't even know the guy, and haven't read any of his other books, but you can just tell that this is the book that he decided to have fun with. And it's a great read. It's hilarious, thoughtful and introspective from time to time, more often absurd. It's a really fun, ridiculous read. As the book progresses, you can't help but shake your head at Coupland's boldness and unashamedne</span>
  <span id="freeText15927247820485514550" style="display:none">It's been a while since I've read this book, so we'll see how well this goes. But this book...is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1886.Douglas_Coupland" title="Douglas Coupland" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Coupland</a>. I don't even know the guy, and haven't read any of his other books, but you can just tell that this is the book that he decided to have fun with. And it's a great read. It's hilarious, thoughtful and introspective from time to time, more often absurd. It's a really fun, ridiculous read. As the book progresses, you can't help but shake your head at Coupland's boldness and unashamedness in bending the rules of fiction, but the end product certainly justifies the means, if I can say that without making any greater philosophical statement.<br /><br />I'm not really part of the generation that this book centers on - I'm a little after their time, I'm afraid - so I'm sure that I didn't connect as well with it as someone who was born a decade earlier might. But as long as you enjoy a good dose of absurdity and a don't mind a plot that doesn't really give a rat's ass where you think it should go next, it's certainly worth a read. It's post-(post-?)modern fiction on the entirely opposite end of the spectrum from the fantastic, elegant, and complex work of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2617.Jonathan_Safran_Foer" title="Jonathan Safran Foer" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Safran Foer</a>, but that is in no way a negative difference. It's absurd where Foer is profound, inscrutable in its complex strangeness rather than its strange complexity. And, in a way, he reminds me, just vaguely, of the great <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Adams</a> - in a totally different genre, of course, but if Adams were to write a book about office drones back home in the early 21st century, I imagine it would look something like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15927247820485514550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109576338" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109576338" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3297204-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266333333p2/3297204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109576338?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/3297204-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109576338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16647028086664938561">When I bought this book, I was with Katie and she was buying a copy of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9915.Less_Than_Zero' title='Less Than Zero by Bret Easton Ellis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Less Than Zero</a> and I said, "In my head, Douglas Coupland and Bret Easton Ellis are the same writer" and she said, "That makes no sense." I think the original random firing of neurons in my brain that connects the two is because they're both vaguely self-hating about being gay, but I've given this more thought and I actually think I'm totally on to something. Both authors wrote allegedly generation defining novels, both love t</span>
  <span id="freeText16647028086664938561" style="display:none">When I bought this book, I was with Katie and she was buying a copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9915.Less_Than_Zero" title="Less Than Zero by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Less Than Zero</a> and I said, "In my head, Douglas Coupland and Bret Easton Ellis are the same writer" and she said, "That makes no sense." I think the original random firing of neurons in my brain that connects the two is because they're both vaguely self-hating about being gay, but I've given this more thought and I actually think I'm totally on to something. Both authors wrote allegedly generation defining novels, both love to use brand names as symbolism and both have self-insertion complex. <br /><br />In the high school of late twentieth/early twenty-first century literature, Douglas Coupland is the shy nerd who's hoping he's going to be able to return for the 20 year reunion a Silicon Valley millionaire with a trophy wife and Bret Easton Ellis is the dreamy enigma who cuts class to smoke cigarettes behind the gym. Meanwhile, they both write books to ensure that these stereotypes continue to exist. Neat!<br /><br />When JPod was first published in 2006, it sounded interesting and I thought about buying it but it was in hardback and really expensive so instead I checked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2748.Microserfs" title="Microserfs by Douglas Coupland" rel="nofollow noopener">Microserfs</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3384.Girlfriend_in_a_Coma" title="Girlfriend in a Coma by Douglas Coupland" rel="nofollow noopener">Girlfriend in a Coma</a> out of the library. I remember that I hated Microserfs because living in the Bay Area on the fringe of the dot-com frenzy in the late 90s was actually kind of gross and I, therefore, did not actually want to read a romanticized book about it. After Girlfriend in a Coma, I wrote elsewhere: <i>I think I'm now prepared to swear off Douglas Coupland forever. Like Bret Easton Ellis except not as funny, Chuck Palahniuk but not as visceral and, of course, let us not forget, an ending that only Christopher Pike could have ghost written.</i><br /><br />(Hah! I was already on to the Douglas Coupland/Bret Easton Ellis connection in 2006 and I don't even think Douglas Coupland was out yet. Actually, with Palahniuk they make a great trio of shifty bundles of internalized homophobia. That's hilarious. In the high school metaphor, Chuck Palahniuk is the guy who gets way too much enjoyment from biology class frog dissection.)<br /><br />BUT ANYWAY: Somehow I forgot that I previously decided I hated Douglas Coupland and I bought JPod and I really liked it. Which is weird, because it's basically the same book as Microserfs. Two theories: 1) Someone from Vancouver might hate this book because of the way it depicts how commercial aspects of American culture migrate (gaming and film industries) migrate to Canada for the tax breaks &amp; 2) I liked JPod more than Microserfs because I'm Generation Y and so I found the humor more relatable. <br /><br />Ugh, that second one is kind of obnoxious but probably true. I think JPod was written before the existence of lolcats, but it has that specific sensibility to its humor: something is funny because it's ironic but what's ironic about it is that it's presented as being completely deadpan and non-ironic and so actually it's just absurd. A lot of the things that happened in this book were like that. <br /><br />Do you think I could pay Chuck Klosterman to write an essay about Bret Easton Ellis, Douglas Coupland and Chuck Palahniuk? Irony would implode. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16647028086664938561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_579181" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/579181" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/579181?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/49806-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Coupland and of moral relativism</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer579181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15390271137247589215">There's a lot to love about this book, and some things that are not so great. Coupland's use of himself as deus ex machina is a little trite, and the ending is unsatisfying to say the least. There are at least 5 plot points raised over the course of the book that are left completely unresolved. It literally just stops. <br/><br/>Still, if you loved Microserfs, all the world weary pop culture references and geek office camaraderie are there for you. <br/><br/>What I really loved about this book was the almost scary </span>
  <span id="freeText15390271137247589215" style="display:none">There's a lot to love about this book, and some things that are not so great. Coupland's use of himself as deus ex machina is a little trite, and the ending is unsatisfying to say the least. There are at least 5 plot points raised over the course of the book that are left completely unresolved. It literally just stops. <br /><br />Still, if you loved Microserfs, all the world weary pop culture references and geek office camaraderie are there for you. <br /><br />What I really loved about this book was the almost scary moral relativism on display (Kam Phong, Carol, Greg, Ethan's dad, Coupland) almost every character does something despicable and/or illegal at some point (murder, adultery, drug trafficking, extortion), and everyone else just kind of shrugs and or aids and abets. It's fascinating. <br /><br />OK, one thing about the end annoyed the hell out of me. Without spoiling, let's just say something important happens towards the end involving digging a hole and a safe deposit box key, and no explanation is given as to why the key is needed. Irritating. <br /><br />Still 4-stars, solely on the basis of well developed characters and first rate geek humor. Highly recommended if you like that sort of thing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15390271137247589215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/63070-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who believes snark's not dead.</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer783455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16356690450595255119">I don't know what anyone expects from Douglas Coupland these days, much less what I expect from the man. I've read everything he's published since Generation X...sometimes I love it, sometimes I loathe it, sometimes I'm just bored.<br/><br/>But this one made me laugh. A lot. And he brings back his plugged-in sense of playful narrative, though I wondered at times if he was sneaking in an imitation of Dave Eggars doing an imitation of Douglas Coupland. If that makes sense.<br/><br/>Or maybe jPod is an all-around shou</span>
  <span id="freeText16356690450595255119" style="display:none">I don't know what anyone expects from Douglas Coupland these days, much less what I expect from the man. I've read everything he's published since Generation X...sometimes I love it, sometimes I loathe it, sometimes I'm just bored.<br /><br />But this one made me laugh. A lot. And he brings back his plugged-in sense of playful narrative, though I wondered at times if he was sneaking in an imitation of Dave Eggars doing an imitation of Douglas Coupland. If that makes sense.<br /><br />Or maybe jPod is an all-around shout-out to his hip-lit contemporaries, being that Coupland himself makes an appearance in the novel as a jaded antagonist, a la Bret Easton Ellis and his infamous meta-shenanigans.<br /><br />Doesn't matter. I laughed. I turned pages with great pleasure. I'll read it again and most likely laugh just as much. Definitely his best attempt in years.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_26027585" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26027585" />
    <a title="Matthew Snope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/142384-matthew-snope"><img alt="Matthew Snope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263251534p2/142384.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26027585?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Snope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Snope" href="/user/show/142384-matthew-snope">Matthew Snope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Matthew by: </span>
      nobody
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26027585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11650642016235948752">I enjoyed this book as I usually enjoy most of Coupland's stuff. But what I really liked are the nonlinear parts of this book that are almost visual art in disguise, especially three rant-ish blocks of Kerouacian flow, in which Coupland nails some dark truths about being alive today and how much the online world influences our individual and collective psyches. I like how he is not just critical of the world surrounding Gen X and Millennial people (like he was in Generation X), but of these gene</span>
  <span id="freeText11650642016235948752" style="display:none">I enjoyed this book as I usually enjoy most of Coupland's stuff. But what I really liked are the nonlinear parts of this book that are almost visual art in disguise, especially three rant-ish blocks of Kerouacian flow, in which Coupland nails some dark truths about being alive today and how much the online world influences our individual and collective psyches. I like how he is not just critical of the world surrounding Gen X and Millennial people (like he was in Generation X), but of these generations themselves. We are complicit in our own misery and shortcomings. If we live in a culture, we contribute to the culture for all its ills. <br />I always feel like I learn something from Coupland's writing, but in a good way rather than your standard academic pedagogical approach. Coupland says the things others are scared or too PC to. He also has a sense of humor coupled with a deep cynicism. Storywise, it seems like he is stuck a bit in a narrative loop and his characters are almost interchangable in all his books/stories. But again what set this book apart is the linear story being made jagged by the inclusion of (it's hard to describe) Coupland's visual sense and the nonlinear pages, which compose a good 20% of the overall book. Pages of HTML code, concepts written in an Asian character-driven language, pages with almost nothing on them but for some little absurd thing, etc. He is showing how the computer and internet are deeply effecting our experience of reality and how our minds think and process the world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11650642016235948752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13032424642252432890">This was middle of the road as far as Coupland books go. People call it Microserfs 2.0. Since I loved Microserfs, I have to disagree.<br/><br/>On one hand, as a computer programmer, I loved that part of the story. The other things I loved? Douglas Coupland portraying himself as a complete douchebag. Ethan's pot-growing, lover-shooting Mom. The quirkiness of the jPod cast.<br/><br/>Now you're asking yourselves "But what did you hate?"   <span id="freeText13032424642252432890" style="display:none">This was middle of the road as far as Coupland books go. People call it Microserfs 2.0. Since I loved Microserfs, I have to disagree.<br /><br />On one hand, as a computer programmer, I loved that part of the story. The other things I loved? Douglas Coupland portraying himself as a complete douchebag. Ethan's pot-growing, lover-shooting Mom. The quirkiness of the jPod cast.<br /><br />Now you're asking yourselves "But what did you hate?" The hatred is directed at the post modern bend the novel takes toward the end. The main characters give each other brain teasers. All of them are wastes of pages. Do we really need 40 or so pages of pi so we can look for the l that he substituted for a 1? That's L and ONE, to make things more legible. The other brainteasers are similar wastes of time.<br /><br />Not to say jPod is a bad book. It isn't. It just isn't as good as a lot of Coupland's others, like Generation X, Microserfs, and All Families are Psychotic. If you're a Coupland completist, you'll still want to read it. Just do your yourself a favor and get it out of the way early.</span>

<div id="review_247016927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/247016927" />
    <a title="Jason Pettus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus"><img alt="Jason Pettus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548377232p2/147289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/247016927?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Pettus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Pettus" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus">Jason Pettus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=hipster">hipster</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=character-heavy">character-heavy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer247016927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2400074114689501965">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br/><br/>As I've detailed here before, I have for most of my adult life been an obsessive fan of "Generation X" phrase-coiner Douglas Coupland; but while I read literally everything from his first book up to <i>Miss Wyoming</i> when younger, mostly for personal reasons, and have read literally everything from <i>The Gum Thie</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2400074114689501965" style="display:none">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br /><br />As I've detailed here before, I have for most of my adult life been an obsessive fan of "Generation X" phrase-coiner Douglas Coupland; but while I read literally everything from his first book up to <i>Miss Wyoming</i> when younger, mostly for personal reasons, and have read literally everything from <i>The Gum Thief</i> to now for professional reasons, there's a chunk from 2000 to 2007 that I completely missed altogether (comprising the books <i>All Families are Psychotic</i>, <i>Hey Nostradamus!</i>, <i>Eleanor Rigby</i> and <i>jPod</i>), mostly because this was when Coupland reached the low point of his transition between Postmodernism and 21st-century "Sincerism," right at a point when I myself was doing a lot more writing of books in my life than the reading of them. I mean, take 2006's <i>jPod</i> as a good example, which was ostensibly meant to be a "conceptual sequel" of sorts to the biggest hit of his career, 1995's <i>Microserfs</i>, with the two novels sharing a lot of the same premises and details; but while <i>Microserfs</i> was a revelatory celebration of a coming geek entrepreneurial class just starting to show itself, <i>jPod</i> is an unimaginative reaction to our Web 2.0 times, with Coupland seemingly out of ideas about what to do with his old pop-culture shtick and quirky Aspie characters besides to ramp things up to an unsatisfyingly cartoonish level, but not yet understanding what he needed to do to change his career path into its next higher level. Eventually, of course, he did end up realizing what to do, which in a nutshell was to make his stories a lot weirder and darker (see <i>Generation A</i> and <i>Player One</i>, for example); but here where he was still floundering with it all, <i>jPod</i> feels very much like a Coupland simply waiting with boredom for the high-profile MTV shorts offer that were guaranteed to come with any early-2000s project of his (and indeed, <i>jPod</i> itself got made into a 13-episode show for Canadian television, with a novel that feels very much like a quickly done afterthought to that show instead of the other way around). As big a fan as I am of his, it's admittedly hard to justify this particular stretch of his career, so best perhaps to turn either to the books older than these or newer to save yourself some wasted reading experiences.<br /><br />Out of 10: <b>N/A</b> </span>
  <a data-text-id="2400074114689501965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_12482446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12482446" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/780845-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12482446?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/780845-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">coupland fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12482446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15286970374093205204">...although a lot of the criticism is warranted. that being said, however, i think what i liked about 'jpod' is coupland's healthy dose of cynacism (a lot of it directed at himself and his celebrity) within the novel's 440 some-odd pages. it's also easy to pass this off as 'microserfs 2.0,' but to do so is a mistake because i think coupland's actually stretched himself like he never has before, not so much in form (because it's almost identical to 'mircoserfs') but rather in tone and theme. most</span>
  <span id="freeText15286970374093205204" style="display:none">...although a lot of the criticism is warranted. that being said, however, i think what i liked about 'jpod' is coupland's healthy dose of cynacism (a lot of it directed at himself and his celebrity) within the novel's 440 some-odd pages. it's also easy to pass this off as 'microserfs 2.0,' but to do so is a mistake because i think coupland's actually stretched himself like he never has before, not so much in form (because it's almost identical to 'mircoserfs') but rather in tone and theme. most of the characters in 'microserfs' were endearing folks, people you genuinely liked. a lot of the characters in 'jpod' are often unlikeable, especially the novel's protagonist, ethan jarlweski (not hard to see why when you see ethan's parents). <br /><br />i do understand some people's abhorrance to inserting onself into one's novel, but the douglas coupland *in* the novel is five-star jerk off. had coupland the author made coupland the character some winning, all-around-good-guy dood, that would have been repulsive. some of the character's are, granted, a bit overdrawn and exaggerated (freedome, kam, steve, evil mark, bree, etc), but couplands endows them with enough fallability and imperfection that rise about the cliched 'quirky and offbeat' characters that pollute so many novels. and since i work in a programming department, i got a kick out of the jpod crew. by and large coupland nails techie and geek culture to the t. i laughed out loud several times. <br /><br />the novel's denouement is something of a let down, but i think this is entirely the point. it would have been relatively easy to wind it up nicely and neatly as he often does. this is where coupland's cynicism comes shining through: in some ways it does come off as a contractual obligation book and i can't help but think it's a subtle jab at his publisher and his readers. some may find this off-putting. i, on the other, found it completely hilarious. too many authors take their 'art' way too seriously. coupland, however, seems to have a different view. why else would he insert twenty pages of prime numbers? lighten up, people. take a joke. coupland's 'plot' in 'jpod' is merely the vehicle for his cynicism and sense of humor. don't take it too seriously. just go with it. enjoy it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15286970374093205204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer5192792" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7850151541487953965">I wanted to like this book, but as with most of Coupland's work these days, it just seemed needlessly convoluted and gimmicky, and was populated with a host of thoroughly unlikable characters. Most everything he writes is the sort of thing that you'd find to be brilliant if you were in your early twenties and looking forward to a life of exciting employment in the gaming/software industry--but anyone who's been around the block in that world a few times would find it to be sophomoric at best, I </span>
  <span id="freeText7850151541487953965" style="display:none">I wanted to like this book, but as with most of Coupland's work these days, it just seemed needlessly convoluted and gimmicky, and was populated with a host of thoroughly unlikable characters. Most everything he writes is the sort of thing that you'd find to be brilliant if you were in your early twenties and looking forward to a life of exciting employment in the gaming/software industry--but anyone who's been around the block in that world a few times would find it to be sophomoric at best, I think. I know, I know, it's supposed to be a wackytime parody--but if so it's a fairly tedious one. <br /><br />I was hoping for a return to the good old days of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2748.Microserfs" title="Microserfs by Douglas Coupland" rel="nofollow noopener"> Microserfs</a> but all this read did was lead me to wonder if <i>Microserfs</i> was as good as I imagined it to be at the time (when I was, er, 24, and looking forward to a life of exciting employment in the software industry). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7850151541487953965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_70988517" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70988517" />
    <a title="Larry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2332179-larry"><img alt="Larry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243169298p2/2332179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70988517?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry" href="/user/show/2332179-larry">Larry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70988517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11875545849844952683">Awesome! This is my first Coupland book - am anxious to read more. <br/><br/>JPod follows the lives of five young co-workers at a large video game design company - working in a soul-less, mindless...but funky...corporate environment where they each stuggle with the shallowness of their worklives. One writes: <br/><br/>"We accept that a corporation determines our life's routines. It's the trade-off so that we don't have to be chronically unemployed creative types, and we know it. When we were younger, we'd at least </span>
  <span id="freeText11875545849844952683" style="display:none">Awesome! This is my first Coupland book - am anxious to read more. <br /><br />JPod follows the lives of five young co-workers at a large video game design company - working in a soul-less, mindless...but funky...corporate environment where they each stuggle with the shallowness of their worklives. One writes: <br /><br />"We accept that a corporation determines our life's routines. It's the trade-off so that we don't have to be chronically unemployed creative types, and we know it. When we were younger, we'd at least make a show of not being fooled and leave copies of Adbusters on our desktops. After a few years it just doesn't matter."<br /><br />On that level, the story is dark and depressing. But Coupland is a humourist, somewhat like Vonnegut. JPod has an ensemble cast of completely wacky characters who face an ongoing series of bizarre and laughable work and non-work situations. And similar to reading Kafka, the social norms and patterns that can deaden our lives are transformed into fodder for a top-notch comedy. A wonderful read! </span>
  <a data-text-id="11875545849844952683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2572477230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2572477230" />
    <a title="Michael Livingston" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1340545-michael-livingston"><img alt="Michael Livingston" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385940570p2/1340545.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2572477230?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Livingston" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Livingston" href="/user/show/1340545-michael-livingston">Michael Livingston</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2572477230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10563007370673469910">The trouble with writing something so zeitgeisty is that within 10 years your books is going to be very, very dated. Coupland is funny and obviously (was) immersed in digital culture at the time, but the story is all over the place plot-wise and the characters are paper thin. This is all a vessel for Coupland's tricksiness - I used to love his flashy nonsense, but this time I was completely underwhelmed. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_337794676" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/337794676" />
    <a title="Jeremy Bibaud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3664938-jeremy-bibaud"><img alt="Jeremy Bibaud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387470722p2/3664938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/337794676?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy Bibaud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy Bibaud" href="/user/show/3664938-jeremy-bibaud">Jeremy Bibaud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3664938-jeremy-bibaud?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer337794676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1690249765749627688">JPod was recommended to me by several friends. Coupland seems to have a very loyal and dedicated following, especially from people of particular generations. So when I first bought JPod I was excited to read an author who would be able to put the nuanced plight of my generation on the page in a fun, contemporary way.<br/><br/>I didn't get that. At all.<br/><br/>I've been told that JPod isn't the best example of his work, but it did enough damage to my perception of his style to cause me not to bother looking furthe</span>
  <span id="freeText1690249765749627688" style="display:none">JPod was recommended to me by several friends. Coupland seems to have a very loyal and dedicated following, especially from people of particular generations. So when I first bought JPod I was excited to read an author who would be able to put the nuanced plight of my generation on the page in a fun, contemporary way.<br /><br />I didn't get that. At all.<br /><br />I've been told that JPod isn't the best example of his work, but it did enough damage to my perception of his style to cause me not to bother looking further. When I give a book one star it's obviously more than a dislike of the topic, technical issues or some other part of the recipe of taste. It's just a piece that didn't work for me on every level.<br /><br />But for the benefit of anyone thinking about reading the book, I'll explain what bothered me most about it and remind you that my strong dislike of the book is obviously not shared by most and so take my opinion as the minority. You might like it a lot more than I did.<br /><br />For me, as I read the book, I literally felt like I was wasting my time. It's one thing to do something just for fun, knowing full well it has no meaning or impact on your life; it's another thing entirely to be doing something you thought would be fun, only to have the conscious realization that every minute that ticks down as you read is a minute stolen from you. And I'm a slow reader so it's even more painful.<br /><br />The characters were paper-thin, none of them likeable. And I know people just like them in real life. But I like those people. So I'm assuming Coupland failed to capture their redeeming characteristics for me. The plot was trivial. The dialogue was trivial. Everything was just that - trivial.<br /><br />It was self-referential, pretentious, and loaded with pop-culture references. Not in a good 'we'll look back at this someday and get nostalgic for the classics' kinda way. It was more of a 'why would he reference something no one will remember in three years?' kinda way.<br /><br />I suppose some might say that everything I've identified may be the whole point of the book and it's a clever statement on our culture or generation. If that's the case, then I was right in feeling like my time was being wasted. And if I'm wrong, then it's just a boring book. Not sure he wins either way with me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1690249765749627688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_146306688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146306688" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146306688?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          marked it as did-not-finish

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4882193-paul?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146306688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3006764958975073174">No rating: did not finish.<br/><br/>Some years ago I read Douglas Coupland's Microserfs and liked it -- it was a penetrating look into the lives of IT workers, told through fictional but clearly based-on-life characters. JPod, however, is mere farce -- absurdity piled on absurdity. The main character is the son of a mother who kills a biker and buries the body in her basement; later, with his mother, he holds up and robs another group of bikers. Through a friend of his brother's, he becomes part of an ill</span>
  <span id="freeText3006764958975073174" style="display:none">No rating: did not finish.<br /><br />Some years ago I read Douglas Coupland's Microserfs and liked it -- it was a penetrating look into the lives of IT workers, told through fictional but clearly based-on-life characters. JPod, however, is mere farce -- absurdity piled on absurdity. The main character is the son of a mother who kills a biker and buries the body in her basement; later, with his mother, he holds up and robs another group of bikers. Through a friend of his brother's, he becomes part of an illegal Chinese immigrant sumggling ring. Page after page is filled with giant type, mere collections of symbols and Microsoft wingding characters, padding out what in fact is a novelette, if that. I don't know what additional absurdities Coupland planned to inflict on me, because I gave up less than a third of the way into the story. Nothing in JPod is in any way connected to life as we know it . . . Coupland is coasting; JPod is not worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3006764958975073174" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3217959-erin-l?shelf=canadian">canadian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2970953212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer675911039520403574">A weird, fun read that made me laugh out loud at the reality of life in an IT pod. :)</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ral6Y9WzuOYxptonWhSy/Cw0h7YxbTV+YOrrSgeo9pwSSVUGkDGtxBk+FXUxitHKS5erR64SCYzClOsgHT64ww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('URTo51jk+ceArDUk3hCYCsiLohlmBqMio6i5A169v0Xu9MeCHWbs5ag0+na1jvs8ryiO6Pl5n9AB1rlpRCvxGg==')})
  }

It is the story of a young man and woman who fall madly in love and whirl through Europe in a frenzied haze of heroin/cocaine adventure. Their ecstacy is brought to an abrupt end when their drug supply is cut off and despair replaces joy. In this distres<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5871868273983800627\" style=\"display:none\">In Guignol\'s Band, first published in France in 1943, Céline explores the horror of a disordered world. The hero, the semi-autobiographical Ferdinand, moves through the nightmare of London\'s underworld during the years of World War I. In this distressing setting, he meets pimps and prostitutes, pawnbrokers and magicians, policemen and arsonists. He sees social and physiological decomposition as these processes unfold along parallel lines of development. The illusions of existence are nakedly exposed. The narrative erupts in Céline\'s characteristic elliptical style. His splintered sentences and scatology reflect his fury at the fragmentation of experience and at his own impotence in the face of it. Out of his rage, he forces the meaninglessness back on itself, and the exuberance of his struggle triumphs in the comic exaggeration of satire. Ultimately, his subject is not death but life, and he responds to it by a strengthened commitment to the sensual and concrete. His hallucinatory world is so vividly realized that it does, indeed, challenge the reality of the reader\'s more conventional world.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5871868273983800627\" href=\"#\"           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7417591706126007434">Douglas Coupland is Canadian, born on a Canadian Air Force base near Baden-Baden, Germany, on December 30, 1961. In 1965 his family moved to Vancouver, Canada, where he continues to live and work. Coupland has studied art and design in Vancouver, Canada, Milan, Italy and Sapporo, Japan. His first novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3378.Generation_X' title='Generation X' rel='nofollow noopener'>Generation X</a>, was published in March of 1991. Since then he has published nine novels and sever</span>
  <span id="freeText7417591706126007434" style="display:none">Douglas Coupland is Canadian, born on a Canadian Air Force base near Baden-Baden, Germany, on December 30, 1961. In 1965 his family moved to Vancouver, Canada, where he continues to live and work. Coupland has studied art and design in Vancouver, Canada, Milan, Italy and Sapporo, Japan. His first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3378.Generation_X" title="Generation X" rel="nofollow noopener">Generation X</a>, was published in March of 1991. Since then he has published nine novels and several non-fiction books in 35 languages and most countries on earth. He has written and performed for the Royal Shakespeare Company in Stratford, England, and in 2001 resumed his practice as a visual artist, with exhibitions in spaces in North America, Europe and Asia. 2006 marks the premiere of the feature film Everything's Gone Green, his first story written specifically for the screen and not adapted from any previous work. A TV series (13 one-hour episodes) based on his novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/221059.jPod" title="jPod" rel="nofollow noopener">jPod</a> premieres on the CBC in January, 2008. <br /><br />--------------------------------------<br /><br /><i>Retrieved 07:55, May 15, 2008, from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.coupland.com/coupland_bio.html">http://www.coupland.com/coupland_bio....</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7417591706126007434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
